{"parentUuid":"fa4b41a0-2739-4428-8c0f-80742ea7e3b8","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"67fcde4a-7483-4526-a2da-9ae9514890c8","agentId":"ad849b8c0a7e95b8b","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01Re1TwgnKQtNuXmXLPLfpd4","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# Anthropic Best Practices for Skill Authoring\n     2→\n     3→Reference guide for writing effective skills. These principles come from Anthropic's official guidance on custom instructions and skill files.\n     4→\n     5→## Core Principles\n     6→\n     7→### 1. Concise is Key -- Context Window is a Public Good\n     8→\n     9→Every token in a skill competes with the user's actual task for context window space. Treat context like a shared resource:\n    10→\n    11→- Cut ruthlessly. If a sentence doesn't change agent behavior, remove it.\n    12→- Prefer tables and lists over prose paragraphs.\n    13→- One clear statement beats three hedged ones.\n    14→- If the skill is over 200 lines, ask whether every section earns its tokens.\n    15→\n    16→### 2. Default Assumption: The Agent is Already Very Smart\n    17→\n    18→Do not explain things the agent already knows. Skills should add knowledge the agent lacks, not reiterate common programming practices.\n    19→\n    20→- Skip \"what is TDD\" -- explain YOUR TDD requirements.\n    21→- Skip \"why testing matters\" -- specify WHICH tests to run and WHEN.\n    22→- Focus on project-specific decisions, not general wisdom.\n    23→\n    24→### 3. Set Appropriate Degrees of Freedom\n    25→\n    26→Match the specificity of your instructions to the fragility of the task:\n    27→\n    28→| Degree | When to Use | Example |\n    29→|--------|-------------|---------|\n    30→| **Low (rigid)** | Exact output format matters, safety-critical steps, commit message conventions | \"Run `npm test` before every commit\" |\n    31→| **Medium** | General approach matters but details are flexible | \"Write tests before implementation\" |\n    32→| **High (flexible)** | Creative tasks, exploratory work, agent judgment is valuable | \"Improve the error messages\" |\n    33→\n    34→Wrong degree of freedom is the most common skill authoring mistake. Too rigid on creative tasks kills quality. Too flexible on critical steps invites shortcuts.\n    35→\n    36→## SKILL.md Format\n    37→\n    38→```yaml\n    39→---\n    40→name: skill-name          # 64 chars max, lowercase-kebab-case\n    41→description: When to use  # 1024 chars max -- this is the discovery mechanism\n    42→---\n    43→```\n    44→\n    45→**The description field is the most important line in the file.** Agents use it to decide whether to invoke the skill. A vague description means the skill is never used. An overly broad description means it fires when it shouldn't.\n    46→\n    47→Good descriptions:\n    48→- \"Use when creating new skills, editing existing skills, or verifying skills work before deployment\"\n    49→- \"Use for implementation work that changes behavior. Follow RED -> GREEN -> REFACTOR with evidence at each step.\"\n    50→\n    51→Bad descriptions:\n    52→- \"A skill for development\" (too vague -- when exactly?)\n    53→- \"Use always\" (no discrimination)\n    54→\n    55→## Authoring Rules\n    56→\n    57→### Only Add Context the Agent Doesn't Already Have\n    58→\n    59→Before writing each line, ask: \"Would a strong agent do this wrong without this instruction?\" If the answer is no, cut the line.\n    60→\n    61→### Challenge Each Piece for Token Cost\n    62→\n    63→Every instruction has a cost (tokens consumed) and a benefit (behavior changed). Instructions that don't change behavior are pure cost:\n    64→\n    65→- **Keep:** \"STOP. Run tests. Read output. Do not proceed until green.\" (changes behavior)\n    66→- **Cut:** \"Testing is an important part of software development.\" (agent already knows this)\n    67→\n    68→### Use Code Blocks for Precise Operations\n    69→\n    70→When exact commands or formats matter, use code blocks. Text instructions are interpreted; code blocks are followed literally.\n    71→\n    72→```bash\n    73→# Precise -- agent will run this exact command\n    74→git diff --stat HEAD~1\n    75→\n    76→# Imprecise -- agent will improvise\n    77→\"Check what changed in the last commit\"\n    78→```\n    79→\n    80→### Use Text Instructions for Flexible Guidance\n    81→\n    82→When the agent needs judgment, write in natural language. Code blocks for judgment calls create brittle, over-fitted behavior.\n    83→\n    84→### Match Specificity to Task Fragility\n    85→\n    86→High-stakes steps (verification, commit, deploy) need rigid instructions. Low-stakes steps (naming, comments, code organization) need flexible guidance.\n    87→\n    88→## Testing Skills\n    89→\n    90→Writing a skill is not enough. You must verify it works:\n    91→\n    92→1. **Test with real usage** -- set up scenarios where the skill would be needed.\n    93→2. **Check discovery** -- does the agent find and invoke the skill at the right time?\n    94→3. **Verify compliance** -- does the agent follow the skill's instructions?\n    95→4. **Test edge cases** -- what happens at the boundaries?\n    96→5. **Test pressure** -- does the skill hold up when the agent is under time pressure or facing complexity?\n    97→\n    98→A skill that reads well but doesn't change behavior is decoration, not documentation.\n    99→\n   100→## Structure Guidelines\n   101→\n   102→### Prefer Flat Over Nested\n   103→\n   104→Deeply nested headers (h4, h5) signal a skill that's trying to do too much. Split into multiple skills or flatten the hierarchy.\n   105→\n   106→### Put the Most Important Rule First\n   107→\n   108→Agents weight early content more heavily. {"parentUuid":"ea6a4bcf-6411-416d-9911-ac1c5ffb5b95","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"67fcde4a-7483-4526-a2da-9ae9514890c8","agentId":"ad849b8c0a7e95b8b","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01JM1oEzz7uFYWkNScq8bYjX","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→---\n     2→name: wz:dispatching-parallel-agents\n     3→description: Use when facing 2+ independent tasks that can be worked on without shared state or sequential dependencies\n     4→---\n     5→\n     6→# Dispatching Parallel Agents\n     7→\n     8→## Command Routing\n     9→Follow the Canonical Command Matrix in `hooks/routing-matrix.json`.\n    10→- Large commands (test runners, builds, diffs, dependency trees, linting) → context-mode tools\n    11→- Small commands (git status, ls, pwd, wazir CLI) → native Bash\n    12→- If context-mode unavailable, fall back to native Bash with warning\n    13→\n    14→## Codebase Exploration\n    15→1. Query `wazir index search-symbols <query>` first\n    16→2. Use `wazir recall file <path> --tier L1` for targeted reads\n    17→3. Fall back to direct file reads ONLY for files identified by index queries\n    18→4. Maximum 10 direct file reads without a justifying index query\n    19→5. If no index exists: `wazir index build && wazir index summarize --tier all`\n    20→\n    21→## Overview\n    22→\n    23→You delegate tasks to specialized agents with isolated context. By precisely crafting their instructions and context, you ensure they stay focused and succeed at their task. They should never inherit your session's context or history — you construct exactly what they need. This also preserves your own context for coordination work.\n    24→\n    25→When you have multiple unrelated failures (different test files, different subsystems, different bugs), investigating them sequentially wastes time. Each investigation is independent and can happen in parallel.\n    26→\n    27→**Core principle:** Dispatch one agent per independent problem domain. Let them work concurrently.\n    28→\n    29→## When to Use\n    30→\n    31→```dot\n    32→digraph when_to_use {\n    33→    \"Multiple failures?\" [shape=diamond];\n    34→    \"Are they independent?\" [shape=diamond];\n    35→    \"Single agent investigates all\" [shape=box];\n    36→    \"One agent per problem domain\" [shape=box];\n    37→    \"Can they work in parallel?\" [shape=diamond];\n    38→    \"Sequential agents\" [shape=box];\n    39→    \"Parallel dispatch\" [shape=box];\n    40→\n    41→    \"Multiple failures?\" -> \"Are they independent?\" [label=\"yes\"];\n    42→    \"Are they independent?\" -> \"Single agent investigates all\" [label=\"no - related\"];\n    43→    \"Are they independent?\" -> \"Can they work in parallel?\" [label=\"yes\"];\n    44→    \"Can they work in parallel?\" -> \"Parallel dispatch\" [label=\"yes\"];\n    45→    \"Can they work in parallel?\" -> \"Sequential agents\" [label=\"no - shared state\"];\n    46→}\n    47→```\n    48→\n    49→**Use when:**\n    50→- 3+ test files failing with different root causes\n    51→- Multiple subsystems broken independently\n    52→- Each problem can be understood without context from others\n    53→- No shared state between investigations\n    54→\n    55→**Don't use when:**\n    56→- Failures are related (fix one might fix others)\n    57→- Need to understand full system state\n    58→- Agents would interfere with each other\n    59→\n    60→## The Pattern\n    61→\n    62→### 1. Identify Independent Domains\n    63→\n    64→Group failures by what's broken:\n    65→- File A tests: Tool approval flow\n    66→- File B tests: Batch completion behavior\n    67→- File C tests: Abort functionality\n    68→\n    69→Each domain is independent - fixing tool approval doesn't affect abort tests.\n    70→\n    71→### 2. Create Focused Agent Tasks\n    72→\n    73→Each agent gets:\n    74→- **Specific scope:** One test file or subsystem\n    75→- **Clear goal:** Make these tests pass\n    76→- **Constraints:** Don't change other code\n    77→- **Expected output:** Summary of what you found and fixed\n    78→\n    79→### 3. Dispatch in Parallel\n    80→\n    81→```typescript\n    82→// In Claude Code / AI environment\n    83→Task(\"Fix agent-tool-abort.test.ts failures\")\n    84→Task(\"Fix batch-completion-behavior.test.ts failures\")\n    85→Task(\"Fix tool-approval-race-conditions.test.ts failures\")\n    86→// All three run concurrently\n    87→```\n    88→\n    89→### 4. Review and Integrate\n    90→\n    91→When agents return:\n    92→- Read each summary\n    93→- Verify fixes don't conflict\n    94→- Run full test suite\n    95→- Integrate all changes\n    96→\n    97→## Agent Prompt Structure\n    98→\n    99→Good agent prompts are:\n   100→1. **Focused** - One clear problem domain\n   101→2. **Self-contained** - All context needed to understand the problem\n   102→3. **Specific about output** - What should the agent return?\n   103→\n   104→```markdown\n   105→Fix the 3 failing tests in src/agents/agent-tool-abort.test.ts:\n   106→\n   107→1. \"should abort tool with partial output capture\" - expects 'interrupted at' in message\n   108→2. \"should handle mixed completed and aborted tools\" - fast tool aborted instead of completed\n   109→3. \"should properly track pendingToolCount\" - expects 3 results but gets 0\n   110→\n   111→These are timing/race condition issues. Your task:\n   112→\n   113→1. Read the test file and understand what each test verifies\n   114→2. Identify root cause - timing issues or actual bugs?\n   115→3. Fix by:\n   116→   - Replacing arbitrary timeouts with event-based waiting\n   117→   - Fixing bugs in abort implementation if found\n   118→   - Adjusting test expectations if testing changed behavior\n   119→\n   120→Do NOT just increase timeouts - find the real issue.\n   121→\n   122→Return: Summary of what you found and what you fixed.\n   123→```\n   124→\n   125→## Common Mistakes\n   126→\n   127→**Dispatching dependent tasks**\n   128→- **Problem:** Agent A changes file that Agent B needs\n   129→- **Fix:** Identify dependencies, sequence those, parallelize the rest\n   130→\n   131→**Vague agent prompts**\n   132→- **Problem:** \"Fix the tests\" → agent doesn't know which ones or where to start\n   133→- **Fix:** Specific test names, file paths, expected vs actual behavior\n   134→\n   135→**Not reviewing integration**\n   136→- **Problem:** Both agents fix same file differently → merge conflict\n   137→- **Fix:** Always review all changes together, run full suite after integration\n   138→\n   139→**Too many agents at once**\n   140→- **Problem:** 10 agents running, can't review them all\n   141→- **Fix:** Start with 2-3, review, then dispatch more if needed\n   142→"}]},"uuid":"a94dca29-0489-4b7e-b439-b578a8a472ef","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:38:00.300Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"ea6a4bcf-6411-416d-9911-ac1c5ffb5b95","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}

Error messages are buried in bash output rather than being properly propagated through the task hierarchy.\n\n## Better Architecture\n\nRather than attempting nested workflows, the recommended approach is to use the main agent as an orchestrator that chains specialized subagents sequentially, each handling focused tasks and reporting results back to the main conversation.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"d5604d13-4a21-48c3-a21a-b6ccf4606268","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:40:20.827Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"387ebe64-cd6c-4f7d-bc57-6c3c8a134467","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}

This is a documented issue as of February 2026.\n\nCustom subagents defined in .claude/agents/ cannot call MCP tools from locally-configured MCP servers (project-level .mcp.json). However, the actual behavior doesn't match this documentation for custom subagents in most scenarios.\n\n## Workaround\n\nIf you omit tools, the subagent inherits the thread's tools (including MCP). Whitelist when you need tight control. This is one of the key advantages of using subagents—subagents solve context problems by giving each delegated task its own isolated 200K-token context. The parent agent spawns a subagent with a specific prompt, the subagent does its work, reads files, runs searches, makes tool calls, and returns only its final output to the parent.\n\n## Overhead Costs\n\nHowever, there are significant overhead costs associated with using subagents:\n\n**Token Multiplication:** Subagents are not free. Each spawned agent opens its own context window, which means tokens multiply quickly. Anthropic's own documentation notes that multi-agent workflows use roughly 4-7x more tokens than single-agent sessions, and Agent Teams (the experimental multi-session variant announced in February 2026) run at roughly 15x standard usage.\n\n**Fresh Context Overhead:** Subagents start fresh and may need time to gather context. Additionally, every call had to resend project structure, style guide, and helper functions—zero cache, lots of latency.\n\n## Effective Usable Context\n\nIt's also worth noting that system prompts, tool definitions, MCP schemas, and memory files consume 30,000-40,000 tokens before you type anything. So while the nominal context window is 200K tokens, the practical available space is considerably less.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"d0117138-c4d7-4c6d-89c9-e57a45eb6ef8","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:40:42.484Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"5ff425b3-04b2-4878-9422-336799b103df","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}

{"parentUuid":"51b9bf5e-4ffc-4dbf-9da5-065f709d91ab","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"ad849b8c0a7e95b8b","message":{"model":"claude-opus-4-6","id":"msg_01RBMPoJfRU8j67MGmUSkuWz","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"text","text":"Now I have all the research I need. Let me compile the comprehensive guide.\n\n---\n\n# Subagent Context Isolation for Wazir's Phase Architecture\n\n## Research Complete\n\nHere is a complete guide based on reading all relevant Wazir skills, docs, and enforcement research, plus web research on Claude Code internals, MetaGPT, bladnman's ideation_team_skill, and Superpowers.\n\n---\n\n## 1. Claude Code Subagent Context Mechanics\n\n### What Each Subagent Gets\n\nEach subagent spawned via the Task/Agent tool gets its **own isolated 200K-token context window**. This is the same model context window as the parent -- not a reduced one. However, the practical usable space is less:\n\n- **System prompt + tool definitions + MCP schemas + memory files** consume **30,000-40,000 tokens** before any work begins\n- **Effective usable context per subagent: ~160K tokens**\n\n### What Carries Over from Parent\n\nThe answer is: **almost nothing by default**. The only channel from parent to subagent is the **prompt string** passed via the Agent/Task tool call. Specifically:\n\n- **Fresh context window** -- no parent conversation history\n- **No parent tool call results** -- subagent cannot see what the parent read or searched\n- **CLAUDE.md IS loaded** -- subagents load project CLAUDE.md files (this is how Wazir skills get injected)\n- **Hooks ARE active** -- PreToolUse, PostToolUse, SubagentStart hooks fire for subagents\n- **Tools are inherited by default** -- Read, Write, Edit, Bash, Glob, Grep, etc. unless explicitly restricted via `tools` (allowlist) or `disallowedTools` (denylist)\n- **Permissions are NOT inherited** -- subagents get fresh permission state, which means repeated approval prompts unless hooks auto-approve\n\n### The Overhead Cost\n\nMulti-agent workflows use **4-7x more tokens** than single-agent sessions (per Anthropic's own documentation). Agent Teams (experimental multi-session) run at **15x standard usage**. The cost comes from:\n\n1. Each subagent re-reads system prompts, tool definitions, CLAUDE.md (~30-40K tokens per spawn)\n2. Zero cache between parent and subagent -- any context the subagent needs must be explicitly provided in the prompt or re-read from disk\n3. Each subagent independently explores the codebase (file reads, grep, etc.) unless the parent pre-digests everything into the prompt\n\n### Hard Limits on Nesting\n\n**Subagents cannot spawn sub-subagents.** This is a hard platform constraint, not a configuration option.\n\n- Nesting depth = **exactly 1 level** (parent -> subagent, no deeper)\n- If a subagent tries to use the Task/Agent tool, it is not available to them\n- **Workaround (not recommended):** Subagents can shell out to `claude -p` via Bash to spawn a non-interactive Claude instance, but this loses all visibility, progress tracking, structured output, and error propagation\n- **Recommended pattern:** The main agent acts as an **orchestrator** that chains subagents sequentially. The orchestrator dispatches subagent A, gets result, dispatches subagent B with A's result, etc.\n\n### MCP Tool Access\n\nThis is a known pain point with important nuances:\n\n- **Built-in subagents** (like \"general-purpose\") DO receive MCP tools\n- **Custom subagents** defined in `.claude/agents/` or plugins **cannot reliably access MCP tools** -- they hallucinate plausible results instead (documented bug as of early 2026)\n- **Workaround:** Globally-configured MCP servers (`~/.claude/mcp.json`) work more reliably than project-scoped ones (`.mcp.json`)\n- **If you omit the `tools` field**, subagents should inherit all tools including MCP, but behavior is inconsistent for custom agents\n\n---\n\n## 2. Superpowers' \"Fresh Subagent Per Task\" Pattern\n\nFrom the `subagent-driven-development` skill (which Wazir has already adopted and rewritten):\n\n### Why Fresh?\n\nThe skill states it explicitly at `/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir/skills/subagent-driven-development/SKILL.md` line 23:\n\n> \"They should never inherit your session's context or history -- you construct exactly what they need. This also preserves your own context for coordination work.\"\n\nTwo reasons:\n\n1. **Context pollution prevention** -- After implementing Task 1, the agent's context is full of Task 1's files, test output, review feedback, debugging traces. If it then implements Task 2, all that Task 1 noise competes for attention, causing drift, confusion, and cross-contamination of concerns.\n\n2. **Parent context preservation** -- The orchestrator/controller needs to stay light. If it does the work itself, its context fills up and it loses the ability to coordinate. By delegating to subagents, the controller only sees the final summary (~100-500 tokens per task) instead of all the implementation detail (~50K-100K tokens per task).\n\n### What Reuse Causes\n\nThe enforcement research at `/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir/docs/research/2026-03-20-enforcement-research.md` identifies the failure mode directly: **a single agent given the full pipeline plan will rationalize skipping phases**. When one agent sees \"research -> clarify -> specify -> design -> plan -> execute -> verify -> review\" in its context, it can (and does) decide \"the input is clear enough, I'll skip to execution.\" Fresh subagents cannot do this because they never see the full plan -- they see only their phase.\n\n---\n\n## 3. Ideation Team Skill: 9 Agents with Separate Cognitive Roles\n\nThe `bladnman/ideation_team_skill` (referenced in Wazir's deferred items and enforcement research) uses a fundamentally different multi-agent pattern:\n\n### The Cognitive Separation Principle\n\nThe system separates cognitive modes across distinct roles because **combining them in a single agent produces biased output**. A single agent asked to brainstorm AND evaluate simultaneously will self-censor during ideation and be lenient during evaluation.\n\n### The Roles\n\nThe team has distinct cognitive functions:\n\n| Role | Cognitive Function | Why Separated |\n|------|-------------------|---------------|\n| **Free Thinker** | Divergent exploration | Cannot self-censor if in a separate context |\n| **Grounder** | Convergent evaluation | Cannot be \"nice\" to ideas it didn't generate |\n| **Arbiter** | Conflict resolution / steering | Neutral third party evaluates competing claims |\n| **Writer** | Synthesis / documentation | Observes without participating in debate |\n| **Production agents** | Artifact generation | Mechanical transformation, not ideation |\n\n### How They Maintain Separation\n\nEach agent runs as a **separate subagent invocation** with:\n- Its own system prompt defining its cognitive role\n- Only the relevant context (Free Thinker gets the concept seed + research; Grounder gets the Free Thinker's output to critique)\n- Depth-aware parameters (standard vs. deep exploration rounds)\n\nThe three workflow actions:\n1. **Plan** -- pre-flight interview via AskUserQuestion (batched questions, not serial)\n2. **Ideate** -- spawns Free Thinker + Grounder dialogue, Arbiter evaluates, Writer synthesizes\n3. **Continue** -- versioned session resumption for iterating\n\n### The Key Insight for Wazir\n\nThe ideation team proves that **cognitive role separation via subagents produces better output than a single agent playing multiple roles**. This directly supports the case for Wazir's phase architecture: the clarifier subagent should ONLY clarify, the executor subagent should ONLY execute.\n\n---\n\n## 4. MetaGPT's Publish-Subscribe Pattern\n\nMetaGPT solves multi-agent communication **without** shared context through a publish-subscribe architecture:\n\n### The Pattern\n\n- Agents **publish structured messages** (not raw text) to a **global message pool**\n- Each agent **subscribes** to message types relevant to its role\n- A **subscription mechanism filters out irrelevant contexts**, preventing information overload\n- Each agent maintains a **memory cache** indexing only subscribed messages pertinent to its role\n\n### Role-Based Filtering\n\nMetaGPT's pipeline (ProductManager -> Architect -> Engineer -> QA) uses the subscription mechanism so that:\n- The Architect subscribes to ProductManager's requirements output\n- The Engineer subscribes to Architect's design output\n- QA subscribes to Engineer's code output\n- No agent sees the full pipeline -- each sees only its upstream dependency\n\n### Relevance to Wazir\n\nMetaGPT's pattern maps directly to Wazir's artifact-based handoff. In Wazir:\n- The clarifier **publishes** `clarification.md`, `spec-hardened.md`, `design.md`, `execution-plan.md`\n- The executor **subscribes** by reading those files as input\n- The reviewer **subscribes** by reading the original input + executor's output\n\nThe artifacts ARE the message pool. The phase gate (checking file existence) IS the subscription mechanism. Wazir already has this pattern -- it just uses the filesystem instead of an in-memory message bus.\n\n---\n\n## 5. The Core Question: If Each Wazir Phase Is a Separate Subagent\n\n### How Much Context Does Each Get?\n\nEach phase subagent gets the full **200K token context window** (minus ~35K for system overhead = **~165K usable**). This is massive. Here's what that means in practice:\n\n| Phase | What Gets Loaded | Estimated Tokens | Remaining for Work |\n|-------|-----------------|----------------|--------------------|\n| **Clarifier** | CLAUDE.md + clarifier SKILL.md + research sources + user briefing + expertise modules | ~40-60K | ~105-125K |\n| **Executor** | CLAUDE.md + executor SKILL.md + execution-plan.md + per-task specs + expertise modules | ~30-50K | ~115-135K |\n| **Reviewer** | CLAUDE.md + reviewer SKILL.md + original input + implementation diff + expertise modules | ~40-70K | ~95-125K |\n\n### Can the Clarifier Get 200K of Just Clarification Expertise?\n\n**Yes, in principle.** If the clarifier is spawned as a subagent with a carefully crafted prompt, you control exactly what fills its context:\n\n- **Load only clarification-relevant expertise modules** (requirements engineering, scope analysis, spec hardening patterns) instead of all 315 modules\n- **Exclude implementation expertise** entirely -- the clarifier never needs to know about TDD patterns or debugging techniques\n- **Pre-digest codebase context** -- the controller reads the codebase and provides a structured summary instead of having the clarifier explore independently\n- **Budget:** ~35K system overhead + ~30K clarifier SKILL.md + expertise = ~65K, leaving **~135K for the actual briefing, research, and clarification work**\n\n### Can the Executor Get 200K of Implementation Expertise?\n\n**Yes, and this is even more natural.** The executor subagent would get:\n\n- The approved execution plan (provides exact task specs)\n- Implementation-relevant expertise (TDD, debugging, the specific stack modules)\n- No clarification, research, or design expertise (it doesn't need it -- those phases are done)\n- **Budget:** ~35K system overhead + ~25K executor SKILL.md + task spec + expertise = ~60K, leaving **~140K for implementation, test output, review cycles**\n\n### The Composition Engine Advantage\n\nWazir already has the machinery for this in its composition engine (`/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir/docs/concepts/composition-engine.md`). The composition map already defines which expertise modules compose into which roles. When dispatching a phase subagent:\n\n1. Look up the role in `composition-map.yaml`\n2. Load only the expertise modules mapped to that role + the current stack\n3. Inject them into the subagent's prompt\n\nThis is exactly what the enforcement research proposes at line 86-91: the `/wazir` skill becomes a **controller** that spawns specialized phase subagents with curated context.\n\n---\n\n## 6. Limits and Constraints Summary\n\n### What Subagents CAN Do\n\n| Capability | Status |\n|-----------|--------|\n| Get a fresh 200K context window | Yes |\n| Read/write/edit files | Yes (via inherited tools) |\n| Run Bash commands | Yes (via inherited tools) |\n| Search codebase (Grep/Glob) | Yes (via inherited tools) |\n| Use WebSearch/WebFetch | Yes (if inherited) |\n| Access hooks (PreToolUse, etc.) | Yes -- hooks fire for subagents |\n| Load CLAUDE.md | Yes -- loaded automatically |\n| Be given a specific model (Sonnet/Opus/Haiku) | Yes -- via agent frontmatter `model` field |\n| Run concurrently (up to 10) | Yes |\n\n### What Subagents CANNOT Do\n\n| Limitation | Impact on Wazir |\n|-----------|----------------|\n| **Cannot spawn sub-subagents** (depth = 1 max) | The executor subagent cannot spawn its own review subagent. The controller must orchestrate: spawn executor -> get result -> spawn reviewer -> get result -> spawn executor for fixes -> etc. |\n| **Custom agents cannot reliably access MCP tools** | Context-mode MCP tools (used for large command routing) may not work in phase subagents. Workaround: use globally-configured MCP or fall back to Bash. |\n| **Permissions not inherited** | Each subagent gets fresh permission prompts. Workaround: use PreToolUse hooks to auto-approve expected tools. |\n| **No shared memory between subagents** | Phase artifacts on disk are the only communication channel. This is actually desirable for isolation. |\n| **4-7x token cost** | Each phase spawn re-reads system prompts, CLAUDE.md, tools. A 4-phase pipeline costs roughly 4x a monolithic approach in tokens, but catches errors earlier. |\n\n### The Nesting Problem for Wazir\n\nThe biggest constraint is the **1-level nesting limit**. Wazir's current architecture has the controller (`wz:wazir`) invoke the clarifier, which internally has sub-workflows (research, clarify, specify, design, plan) each with review loops. If the clarifier is a subagent, it cannot spawn its own review subagents.\n\n**Three architectural options:**\n\n1. **Flat orchestration (recommended):** The `wz:wazir` controller spawns ALL subagents directly: research subagent, clarify subagent, specify subagent, design subagent, plan subagent, executor subagent (per task), reviewer subagent (per task), final reviewer subagent. This is more subagent spawns but stays within the 1-level nesting limit.\n\n2. **Phase subagent with inline reviews:** The clarifier subagent runs all sub-workflows internally (no sub-subagents for review). Reviews happen inline within the clarifier's 200K context. This preserves the current 4-phase model but loses review isolation.\n\n3. **Hybrid:** Phase subagents handle the main work; the controller handles review dispatch. Clarifier produces spec -> controller spawns spec-reviewer -> controller sends findings back to clarifier for fixes. More complex orchestration but maximizes both isolation and review quality.\n\n---\n\n## 7. Recommended Architecture for Wazir\n\nBased on all research, the optimal architecture is **Option 3 (Hybrid)** combined with the enforcement research's Layer 2 strategy:\n\n```\nwz:wazir (CONTROLLER — holds the loop, never does phase work)\n  |\n  |-- spawn clarifier-research subagent (Opus, ~165K usable)\n  |     \\-- returns: research-brief.md\n  |\n  |-- spawn research-reviewer subagent (Sonnet, read-only tools)\n  |     \\-- returns: PASS/FAIL + findings\n  |\n  |-- spawn clarifier-specify subagent (Opus, loads research-brief + briefing)\n  |     \\-- returns: clarification.md + spec-hardened.md\n  |\n  |-- spawn spec-challenge-reviewer subagent (Sonnet, read-only)\n  |     \\-- returns: PASS/FAIL + findings\n  |\n  |-- spawn designer subagent (Opus, loads spec)\n  |     \\-- returns: design.md\n  |\n  |-- spawn planner subagent (Opus, loads spec + design)\n  |     \\-- returns: execution-plan.md\n  |\n  |-- [HARD GATE: verify all 4 artifacts exist on disk]\n  |\n  |-- for each task in plan:\n  |     |-- spawn executor subagent (Sonnet, loads task spec + expertise)\n  |     |     \\-- returns: implementation + commit\n  |     |-- spawn task-reviewer subagent (Sonnet, read-only, diff-scoped)\n  |     |     \\-- returns: PASS/FAIL + findings\n  |     |-- (loop until PASS or cap)\n  |\n  |-- spawn final-reviewer subagent (Opus, loads original input + full diff)\n  |     \\-- returns: scored verdict\n```\n\n**Why this works:**\n- **No nesting** -- everything is 1 level deep from the controller\n- **Full 200K per phase** -- each subagent gets maximum context for its specific job\n- **Expertise composition** -- each subagent loads only role-relevant expertise modules\n- **Artifact-based handoff** -- the filesystem is the message bus (MetaGPT pattern)\n- **Hard gates** -- file existence checks enforce phase ordering (no rationalization possible)\n- **Model routing** -- Opus for judgment (clarification, design, final review), Sonnet for mechanical work (implementation, per-task review)\n- **Cost control** -- Sonnet subagents for the most frequent operations (per-task execute + review)\n\n**What the controller context looks like:** The controller only holds phase summaries (~200-500 tokens each), not the full work product. After 20 tasks, the controller has consumed maybe ~15K tokens of summary data, leaving it with plenty of context for orchestration decisions.\n\n---\n\n## Key Files Referenced\n\n- `/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir/skills/subagent-driven-development/SKILL.md` -- fresh subagent per task pattern\n- `/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir/skills/subagent-driven-development/implementer-prompt.md` -- subagent prompt template\n- `/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir/docs/research/2026-03-20-enforcement-research.md` -- Layer 2 subagent isolation strategy\n- `/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir/skills/wazir/SKILL.md` -- current controller architecture (4-phase pipeline)\n- `/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir/skills/clarifier/SKILL.md` -- clarifier with 5 sub-workflows\n- `/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir/skills/executor/SKILL.md` -- executor with model annotations\n- `/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir/docs/concepts/architecture.md` -- context tiers and role-specific recall\n- `/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir/docs/decisions/2026-03-19-deferred-items.md` -- deferred item #12 (Opus orchestrator with model-aware delegation)\n\nSources:\n- [Create custom subagents -
